Through intellect, one contemplates or 'sees' the Absolute; through reason alone, one can only think about it.[^cite-80] This distinction proves essential for understanding the spiritual ethos we examine here.

Three factors are inherent to the intellectual perspective being considered. First: the centrality of revelation. Second: the harmony between revelation and intellect. Third: the distinction between intellect (conceived as the principle articulating consciousness) and reason. Each demands our attention.

Maulana Ali's entire life and teaching center upon the Qur'anic revelation and its embodiment in the Prophet's conduct. Both his thought and his life may be read as a kind of spiritual commentary—a *taʾwīl* (esoteric exegesis)—upon the sacred text.[^cite-81] On the doctrinal plane, it can be asserted with confidence that Maulana Ali possessed an innate aptitude for performing taʾwīl. His very existence unfolded as a dramatic realization of the inner meaning of revelation: from his birth within the most holy shrine of the Kaaba to his martyrdom in the mosque of Kufa. His refusal to rest at the superficial surface of things—whether religious observance, ethics, or politics—manifested an unwavering fidelity to the sacred substance of the religion. This fidelity demanded both inward intellectual penetration and outward combative resistance. The Prophet (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) said of him: "Ali will fight for the *taʾwīl* of the Qur'an as I fought for its *tanzīl* (revelation)."[^cite-82]

Maulana Ali's brief caliphate was consumed by three bloody civil wars. Yet all three may be understood as having been fought for the correct interpretation and application of the Qur'an, the clarification of its fundamental message (*tawḥīd*, the affirmation of divine oneness), and the realization of this oneness on all spiritual and social levels. This is illustrated graphically in Maulana Ali's response to a bedouin who, as the Battle of the Camel (al-Jamal) was about to commence, approached him asking, "O Commander of the Faithful! Do you say that Allah is one?" Those present remonstrated, insisting this was neither the time nor place for such inquiry. Yet Maulana Ali intervened: "Leave him, for surely what the bedouin wishes is what we wish for the people."

This response reveals a principle of paramount importance: right intention takes priority over outward action, whatever that action may be. For Maulana Ali, the fundamental intention underlying every deed has been knowledge of the true nature of ultimate reality. Whether his action be conventionally religious or military, the deepest motive remains identical: knowledge of the divine. What Maulana Ali "wishes for the people," then, is precisely this knowledge—knowledge that must lie at the very heart of all concerns, motivations, and actions, whether on the battlefield or at prayer. There exists no time or place where the question "What do you mean by saying Allah is one?" becomes irrelevant or out of place. It was for the sake of correct knowledge of the divine that Maulana Ali fought; thus too did he fulfill the Prophet's prophecy.

On the very field of battle, Maulana Ali delivered a discourse on the meaning of divine oneness. The opening of his reply contains a key to understanding his entire spiritual ethos. He refutes the notion that Allah's oneness consists in any "numerical" or "countable" singularity. He tells the bedouin: "It is not permissible to say 'one' while having in mind any numerical conception, for that which has no second does not enter into the category of numbers."

"That which has no second" signifies not merely a divinity to which no other partner can be ascribed, but rather that Reality which possesses no 'otherness' whatsoever. The Real (*al-Ḥaqq*, which is at once transcendent and immanent, beyond all things yet manifested through all things, simultaneously inaccessible and inalienable)—in these few words Maulana Ali effects a shift of consciousness, a fundamental reorientation for those with "ears to hear": from the dogmatic notion of one God in opposition to many gods, to the mystical apprehension of the one and only Reality, in relation to which all else is but appearance. All things become perceived as outward phenomena crying out to be deciphered—many signs (āyāt) pointing to that of which they are manifestations. The very meaning of *taʾwīl* is the retracing of these appearances back to their source and origin: a return to the *Awwal* (the First, the Beginning). On one hand, the Real is described as the beginning and end of all things, the apparent and the hidden:

⟪quran 57:3⟫
⟪ar:‏هُوَ ٱلْأَوَّلُ وَٱلْآخِرُ وَٱلظَّهِرُ وَٱلْبَاطِنُ ۖ وَهُوَ بِكُلِّ شَىْءٍ عَلِيمٌ ‎⟫

He is the First and the Last, the Outward and the Inward. And He is the All-Knower of every thing.[^cite-83]

On the other hand, apparent multiplicity is to be "brought back" to real oneness. As Allah declares:

⟪quran 41:53⟫
⟪ar:‏سَنُرِيهِمْ آيَتِنَا فِى ٱلأٓفَاقِ وَفِىٓ أَنفُسِهِمْ حَتَّىٰ يَتَبَيَّنَ لَهُمْ أَنَّهُ ٱلْحَقُّ ۗ أَوَلَمْ يَكْفِ بِرَبِّكَ أَنَّهُۥ عَلَىٰ كُلِّ شَىْءٍۢ شَهِيدٌ ‎⟫

We will show them Our Signs in the universe, and in their ownselves, until it becomes manifest to them that this is the truth.[^cite-84]

In one saying attributed to Maulana Ali, the Prophet is referred to as the first of those taught by Allah the science of *taʾwīl*, and the Prophet then taught Maulana Ali. According to the Prophet: "Truly, Ali is with the Qur'an, and the Qur'an is with Ali."[^cite-85] In another saying, Maulana Ali claimed to be able to load seventy camels with pages of commentary on Surah al-Fātiḥa. His cousin Ibn Abbas testified that he had learned the science of interpretation from Ali.

The following utterance of Maulana Ali proves of fundamental importance:

"The Qur'an consists of a book inscribed between two covers; it speaks not with a tongue, it cannot do without an interpreter (⟪ar:لا بُدَّا مِنَ التَّرجُمٰن⟫)."

This exchange between revelation and intellect is further underscored in another saying:

"The head of a man is the interpreter of his intellect (⟪ar:رَاسُ الرَّجَل تَرجُمان عَقلِہ⟫)."

Elsewhere, we find an explicit correspondence between the intellect and the 'inner' messenger: the intellect is "the messenger of the Real." Maulana Ali's teachings, which express his spiritual ethos, must therefore be read essentially as creative interpretations of Qur'anic revelation. They initiate a movement from the letters of revelation toward its spirit; and from the spirit of revelation toward the spirit of the Revealer:

Letters of revelation → Spirit of Revelation → Revealer (*al-Ḥaqq*)

This process of 'retracing'—of returning to the source, the origin, the beginning, *al-Awwal*—will be accomplished insofar as the spirit of the intellect is enlivened within the interpreter. Yet the Revealer remains inevitably concealed (as the essence of the divine Reality) even as He is revealed through revelation. The divine Reality in itself is too great to be described in words. Only the forms of its manifestations are susceptible to revelation. This dialectical interplay between form and formlessness, the evident and the hidden, the revealed and the concealed, and all such antinomies, lie at the root of the paradoxical mode of expression for which Maulana Ali is renowned.[^cite-86]

One of the most striking aspects of Maulana Ali's perspective is its revelation of the hollowness of many false or excessively polarized dichotomies that later came to characterize—or be imposed upon—Islamic thought. The dichotomy between intellect and revelation appears in various ramifications: reason versus faith, convention (*sunna*) versus nature (*ṭabīʿa*), imitation (*taqlīd*) versus verification (*taḥqīq*), piety (*taqwā*) versus inquiry. In Maulana Ali's ethos, however, intellect and revelation are not regarded as mutually exclusive; rather, they are complementary and, at the highest level, inseparable, for truth is one. While it is clear that the human intellect cannot be equated with divine revelation, the spirit animating the intellect transcends human limitations. It is this spirit which perceives and grasps the divine quality of revelation.

The divine spirit articulating the form and infusing the content of revelation must be, to some degree, understandable to human consciousness. Were this not so, revelation would be nothing more than arbitrary rules and regulations imposed upon the will, rather than a source of creative inspiration for the intellect. Divine revelation presupposes a creative engagement with all the resources of the intellect, rather than the suppression of reason or the imposition of some unintelligible decree. The Qur'an itself repeatedly calls for precisely this engagement.

The revealed text is silent and 'speaks' only through the 'interpreter'—the intellect. This view of the relationship between intellect and revelation rigorously excludes all literalism and superficiality. It excludes all attempts to confine meaning within a flat, unilateral reading. Instead, it tends toward the very opposite: rather than enclosing meaning, it seeks to 'dis-close' it, to open the inner horizons of multiple levels of meaning hidden in the text. This multifaceted significance is explored in accordance with the depth and penetration of the intellect of the 'interpreter.' This 'prophetic' intellect rejoins the source of revelation itself. For it is this 'inner prophet'—this inmost degree of consciousness—which constitutes the ultimate basis upon which one is able to confirm as true the verses of the outer revelation. It 'validates' them experientially, not merely affirming them dogmatically. Thus Maulana Ali states in the first sermon of the Nahj al-Balāgha that one purpose of Allah's "sending forth of prophets" to mankind was "to unearth for them the buried treasures of the intellects (⟪ar:دفائن العقول⟫)".[^cite-87]

This saying constitutes one of the keys to the spiritual ethos being considered here: the intellect is not stifled by revelation; rather, it is enlivened by it. Revelation is a powerful and clear summons to the spiritual depths of the intellect. It cultivates and brings to fruition the seeds of consciousness that Allah has planted therein. The spirit of one's intellect, then, cannot be alien to that which descends as revelation from Allah. On the contrary, it must be fundamentally similar to it. It is precisely for this reason that one can possess absolute certainty that revelation indeed comes from the Absolute. The question is not one of setting up a dichotomy between intellect and revelation, but rather of perceiving the intellect 'as' revelation, or at least as one mode of revelation. The intellect is the God-given means by which the individual comes to discern, affirm, and plumb the divine 'revelations' outside himself—both the signs (*āyāt*) of scripture and the signs (*āyāt*) constituted by the natural world. If the human intellect can perceive and recognize revelation, it is because both are rooted in the same ultimate source. Prophetic revelation awakens and brings to life the depths of human intellect; it does not replace or displace the intellect. From this perspective, the revealed text is not some extraneous source opposed to intelligence, but rather the objective, outward expression of the very principles that the intellect itself yields to the 'inner prophet.' Understanding by 'intellect,' then, does not mean simply the faculty of reasoning, but rather the source of consciousness articulating the human spirit.

The intellect contains unsuspected depths—'buried treasures'—and is thus far from being exhausted by its surface operations or by the cognitions and reasonings proper to its purely mental dimension. These reflections lead us back to the relationship between intellect and reason. Just as there can be no contradiction whatsoever between **revelation** and **intellect**, likewise one must avoid setting up a false dichotomy between **reason** and **intellect**. Instead, reason should be perceived as one of the modes of the intellect. Reason does not contradict intellect, any more than intellect contradicts revelation. Reason is a mode of the intellect, but it is not to be equated with it. Similarly, one cannot reduce the universal objectivity of revelation to the subjective specificities of any individual's intellect, or to his or her own 'private' revelation. To equate reason with intellect would be tantamount to denying the existence of the 'buried treasures' of the intellect—those dimensions that transcend rationality and are initially 'buried' beneath, and thus hidden from, the rational surface of the intellect. The revelation constituted by the intellect is embedded within the creation of the human being. And it is this intellect which most critically defines the human being as such.

Rather than opposing intellect to revelation, each is to be seen in the light of the other. The substance of revelation cannot be engaged except through the intellect on one hand, and the 'buried treasures' of the intellect cannot be disclosed except through revelation on the other. This understanding brings us close to what Maulana Ali refers to as the 'heart'—that innermost mode of perception capable of 'seeing' Allah. It is the 'heart' that can 'see' Allah, not through eyesight but through the spiritual insight generated by the 'true principles of faith' (*ḥaqāʾiq al-Īmān*). As Maulana Ali puts it, responding to one who asked whether he had 'seen' his Lord: "Eyes see Him not through sight's observation, but hearts see Him through the true principles and beliefs of faith."[^cite-88]

Returning to the relationship between intellect and reason: between the spiritual depths of the intellect and its rational surface there exists both continuity and discontinuity. The aspect of continuity is evident in the fact that all thinking begins with concepts conveying intelligible meaning. Thought is initially concerned with ideas that are rationally discernible, and these must bear some relation to the realities they express. But there is also discontinuity. Reason stops short at ideas that have been 'de-fined'—the Arabic word for definition, *ḥadd* (the upper limit), expresses both the sense of a conceptual definition and that of a limitation, a boundary. To go beyond the limits imposed by the rational act of definition requires something more than reason; and this 'something more' is that which renders the ethos we are considering a spiritual one, not a rationalistic one. The ethos is certainly 'rational' in form, susceptible to reason's scrutiny, but it is not 'rationalistic' insofar as its essence is not predicated on reason alone.

Maulana Ali frequently alludes, in highly suggestive terms, to the mysteries and marvels—the unearthed treasures of the intellect—that he has directly witnessed through the eye of his purified heart. He also indirectly alludes to this supra-rational mode of awareness through the bewildering use of apophatic discourse (knowledge of God obtained through negation) and paradoxical utterances, abundantly found in works attributed to him. In this ethos, the higher the truth, the deeper is the paradox. The incommensurability between reality in itself and the human conceptions made of it is effectively evoked through this creative use of paradox. Each human conception of reality needs first to be 'deconstructed' in order then to be 'reconstructed.' Maulana Ali's paradoxes feed directly into modes of spiritual intuition, contemplation, and realization of that which reason, on its own account, is incapable of attaining. It is this intuition and contemplation which 're-members' or 'reconstructs' the spiritual realities that transcend the categories of reason. The value of paradoxes, antinomies (contradictions between two beliefs or conclusions that are in themselves reasonable), apophasis, and negation is entirely predicated upon the various positive realities which the intellect can plumb, thereby becoming aware of its own hidden depths. The resulting consciousness does not negate reason so much as build upon it, revealing the limits of reason's proper sphere of operation. It reveals that reason alone cannot 'comprehend' the higher realities or express them fully, no matter how indispensable reason may be in initiating the cognitive movement toward those realities.

When Maulana Ali negates the possibility of human conceptions encompassing the divine Reality, what is being negated is the limitation which is the inescapable corollary (forming a proposition that follows from one already proved) of all such conceptions, and not the positive content—the *maʿnā* (spiritual meaning)—of the conception itself. This conception is a necessary starting point for a process of assimilation that engages a deeper level of awareness than that 'defined' by the mind, extending far beyond the domain of speculative thought. In this light, the negation of the limitations of human conception should be seen as the introduction to an absolute affirmation: the affirmation of the Absolute, which has no limitation. The spiritual negation of conceptual limitation is of an upward, 'constructive' order, not of a nihilistic (rejecting all religious and moral principles in the belief that life is meaningless) deconstructive one. This style of discourse appears nowhere more strikingly than in the opening lines of the first sermon of the Nahj al-Balāgha:

> Praise be to God, whose praise those who speak cannot deliver, whose graces those who count cannot number, whose rightful due those who strive cannot render; He who cannot be grasped by aspirations, nor fathomed by profound intuitions; He whose attribute has no binding limitation, no existing description, no time appointed, no term extended.

The divine attributes (*sifāt*) cannot be confined to a definition—*laysa li sifātihī ḥaddun maḥdūd*—and yet they are affirmed as real. Allah is described (*mawṣūf*) by the very words of Qur'anic revelation, even though He has no 'description.' For example, Allah is described as the Merciful, the Compassionate, the Almighty. Yet Maulana Ali affirms that these very attributes cannot be defined, bounded, or described. The paradox deepens when Maulana Ali speaks of the perfection of *ikhlāṣ* (rendering sincere; the purification of one's devotion to and conception of Allah). It is to divest (*nafi*, negation) Allah of all attributes. Thus on one hand the attributes are affirmed, and on the other they are to be negated. The rational mind can think of Allah only in terms of the attributes verbally expressed in the Qur'an. Yet here, Maulana Ali appears to negate these same attributes, thereby depriving the mind of any convenient conceptual handle by which to grasp the divine Reality. This simultaneous affirmation and negation of the attributes raises an apparently irresolvable contradiction. But in doing so, it enhances receptivity to a deeper mode of knowing. This style of discourse is designed to awaken a latent awareness, inscribed in the heart or in the depths of the intellect. This awareness invites the seeker to "dive" into the depths of the knowledge 'buried' in the intellect. The very shock of the apparent contradiction precipitates a leap of consciousness from the formal activity of reason to the super-rational contemplation of the spirit.[^cite-89]

Consider the words spoken by Maulana Ali immediately after affirming that his heart had indeed witnessed Allah, not by means of outward vision but through 'the realities of faith':

"My Lord is not described in terms of dimension (⟪ar:بُعْد⟫), nor motion (⟪ar:حرکۃ⟫), nor rest (⟪ar:سکون⟫) nor through coming or going. He is subtle in subtlety but not described by subtlety. He is magnificent in magnificence but not described by magnificence. He is great in greatness but not described by greatness."

The attributes are again affirmed and denied. Allah possesses these attributes, but is not reducible to them. We can begin to think of Allah in terms of these categories, but we cannot reduce Him to our limited conceptions. The attributes surely provide something to the mind by which the divine nature can be understood. But this understanding is fragmentary and never exhaustive. It must therefore be negated, so that a fuller understanding may be gained. The initial conceptual orientation toward the divine Reality needs to be both negated and sublimated. It needs to be negated as a mental conception, while the positive content of the conception needs to be elevated to a high rank through spiritual intuition. Only then can the two-dimensional 'portrait' defined by reason be given the third dimension of depth. But this third dimension emerges only after the insufficiency of the rational depiction is clearly understood. Paradox produces this cognitive shock, revealing the pretension of reason in its effort to disclose the nature of the Absolute.

In Maulana Ali's discourses, there is a repeated combination of affirmation and denial, which mirrors the negation and affirmation found in the first testimony of Islam: *Lā ilāha* (no god, the negation or *nafi*); then *illā Allāh* (except Allah, the affirmation or *isbaāt*). This helps us to see that the perfection of *ikhlāṣ*, as described by Maulana Ali, is gained through the negation of the attributes 'in order to' affirm the infinitude of the divine Reality. Thus each attribute is to be 'negated,' but only insofar as it is conceived by the limited mind. Negation is necessary because at the mental level each attribute 'excludes' not only the other attributes but also the object of attribution itself—the divine Essence. For any concept to develop in the mind, defined ideas are required. And where there are definitions, there are limits, which result in exclusions. However, the reality of the attribute is unlimited by virtue of its absolute identity with the Essence. The attribute that has been 'negated' is paradoxically affirmed as being nothing 'but' Allah: *illā Allāh*. Thus one can place each negated attribute in the place of the negation (*nafi*) of the first testimony, arriving at such formulations as: *Lā Raḥmāna illā Allāh* ("There is no All-Compassionate except Allah"); *Lā Raḥīma illā Allāh* ("There is no All-Merciful except Allah").[^cite-90]

In this respect, the apparent negation of the attributes not only becomes an affirmation of their true identity but also disconnects any restrictive association with the poverty of mental conceptions. The attributes are not superimposed upon the Essence, but are in fact absolutely one with it. The attributes are differentiable from the Essence only in conception, not in existence. The Essence does indeed comprise all the mentioned attributes—greatness, subtlety, and so on—but it is also infinitely more than the sum of these attributes. These attributes are infinite only within and as the Essence. Maulana Ali's explanation of this paradox constitutes, in fact, a commentary on the Qur'anic verse:

⟪quran 23:91⟫
⟪ar:‏مَا ٱتَّخَذَ ٱللَّهُ مِن وَلَدٍۢ وَمَا كَانَ مَعَهُۥ مِنْ إِلَهٍ ۚ إِذًۭا لَّذَهَبَ كُلُّ إِلَهٍۭ بِمَا خَلَقَ وَلَعَلَا بَعْضُهُمْ عَلَىٰ بَعْضٍۢ ۚ سُبْحَنَ ٱللَّهِ عَمَّا يَصِفُونَ ‎⟫

Allah has not begotten a son, nor is there any god besides Him; if there were, each god would have taken away what he had created, and some of them would have risen above others. Glory be to Allah, that which they describe.[^cite-91]

The infinitude of Allah requires the negation of all that the mind can conceive through reason on the human plane. But it does not negate all that the heart can 'see' through spiritual vision. This negation on the plane of reason calls out to something deeper. This in turn can only be understood in the light of spiritual practice, which is always assumed as the reflective, devotional, or contemplative context for the hearers of Maulana Ali's sermons and discourses. The heart—the symbolic seat of the contemplative intelligence—calls out to be 'polished,' and this is precisely what the 'remembrance of Allah' (*Dhikr Allāh*) does. This is why, according to Maulana Ali, the remembrance of Allah is "the most excellent form of worship."[^cite-92]

Interestingly, even at the level of practice there is a further paradox. The 'reality of the remembrance' is not attained until 'you forget yourself' in your remembrance of Allah. It is not merely the rational mind and its concepts that are to be negated or 'deconstructed.' Rather, it is the individual 'soul' itself—as the locus of all cognition, rational or supra-rational—that is to be negated or 'deconstructed.' This final denouement, or annihilation (*fanāʾ*), is the ultimate form of paradox.

In Maulana Ali's perspective, the intellect must be seen, first and foremost, as a spiritual faculty. And the spirit must be appreciated as not only the source of the intellect but also the source of life itself. Without the intellect the spirit is reduced to an unconscious existence. Conversely, without the spirit, the intellect tends toward lifeless thoughts. This is why the Qur'an, in describing the creation of man, states that Allah 'breathed' His spirit into Adam:

⟪quran 32:7⟫
⟪ar:‏ٱلَّذِىٓ أَحْسَنَ كُلَّ شَىْءٍ خَلَقَهُۥ ۖ وَبَدَأَ خَلْقَ ٱلْإِنسَنِ مِن طِينٍۢ ‎⟫

Who made everything He has created good, and He began the creation of man from clay.

⟪quran 32:8⟫
⟪ar:‏ثُمَّ جَعَلَ نَسْلَهُۥ مِن سُلَلَةٍۢ مِّن مَّآءٍۢ مَّهِينٍۢ ‎⟫

Then He made his offspring from semen of worthless water.

⟪quran 32:9⟫
⟪ar:‏ثُمَّ سَوَّىٰهُ وَنَفَخَ فِيهِ مِن رُّوحِهِۦ ۖ وَجَعَلَ لَكُمُ ٱلسَّمْعَ وَٱلْأَبْصَرَ وَٱلْأَفْـِٔدَةَ ۚ قَلِيلًۭا مَّا تَشْكُرُونَ ‎⟫

Then He fashioned him in due proportion, and breathed into him the soul, and He gave you hearing, sight and hearts. Little is the thanks you give![^cite-93]

The following passage from the first sermon of the Nahj al-Balāgha exemplifies Maulana Ali's commentary on this infusion of divine spirit into humanity:

> Then Allah (may He be glorified), gathered up a quantity of dust, from some hard earth and some soft, some sweet earth and some salty, and poured water onto it until it was pure, then kneaded into it some moisture until it stuck together. He then fashioned from this, a form with curves and joints, limbs and parts. He compacted it until it held tightly together. He solidified it until it became as sounding clay, assigning to it a measured duration and a known consummation. Then He breathed into it of His spirit, and it stood up as a human being, endowed with intellectual powers with which to reflect, thoughts by which to conduct himself, limbs to put to service, instruments at his disposal, and knowledge with which to discern between the true and the false.

In other words, it is the 'in-spiration'—literally, the 'in-breathing' and the insertion of the spirit—of the breath of Allah's spirit that produces consciousness and life. Death, by contrast, is the 'ex-piration,' the departure of the spirit. Life, in the absence of fidelity to one's spirit, becomes a kind of death. Through this spirit, inanimate and unconscious matter is brought to life and given awareness of itself. It not only generates knowledge of other forms but also generates knowledge of the self. This intimate intertwining between the gift of life and the capacity to know—existence which is conscious, and knowledge which is existential—constitutes one of the foundations of any truly 'spiritual' perspective. The matter is not one of contrasting spirit with body, but of recognizing that the infusion of the spirit 'into' the body brings the body to life. It is for this reason that 'spiritual' knowledge enlivens the hearts:

⟪quran 8:24⟫
⟪ar:‏يَٓأَيُّهَا ٱلَّذِينَ آمَنُوا۟ ٱسْتَجِيبُوا۟ لِلَّهِ وَلِلرَّسُولِ إِذَا دَعَاكُمْ لِمَا يُحْيِيكُمْ ۖ وَٱعْلَمُوٓا۟ أَنَّ ٱللَّهَ يَحُولُ بَيْنَ ٱلْمَرْءِ وَقَلْبِهِۦ وَأَنَّهُۥٓ إِلَيْهِ تُحْشَرُونَ ‎⟫

O you who believe! Answer Allah (by obeying Him) and (His) Messenger when he (PBUH) calls you to that which will give you life, and know that Allah comes in between a person and his heart (i.e. He prevents an evil person to decide anything). And verily to Him you shall (all) be gathered.[^cite-94]

According to Maulana Ali, one of the most important aspects of this 'call' from Allah to human beings is to 'remind' them of the buried treasures of the intellect.

Existence is the bestowal of the conscious spirit upon the body. Hence the *ʿAql* (intellect), which is the principle of articulation for the spirit, is not merely a mental faculty; it is the very constitution of our true life. The mental faculty is only one mode of the intellect's operation, but by no means the only one. According to the sayings of Maulana Ali, the *ʿAql* is multifaceted, comprising aspects that in our times are rarely associated with intelligence.

Being 'spiritual,' then, means being true to one's intellect—to the treasures buried deep within it—and not merely to the rational functions operating on its surface. For Maulana Ali, the 'true intellectual' (*al-ʿāqil*) is the one who not only 'thinks' correctly but also 'acts' ethically, and at the deepest level seeks to 'realize' the ultimate Reality. He defines the intellectual as "one who puts all things in their proper place." And because this is the very definition of justice also, only the true intellectual can therefore be fully 'just.' Only one who has the ability to see things as they 'truly are' is able to put them in their right place. Therefore, thought, action, and realization are all concerns of the true intellectual—the *ʿāqil*. Correct thinking, impeccable virtue, and authentic being are inseparably bound up with the intellect in this spiritual ethos. These aspects of the ethos become clearer in the light of Maulana Ali's discourse to Kumayl, to which we now turn our attention.


<!-- section 25 (id=5713, raw_sort=25): The Discourse To Kumayl -->

## The Discourse to Kumayl

This discourse has been the subject of numerous commentaries throughout the centuries, its importance lying, among other things, in its highly esoteric character. The Commander of the Faithful takes one of his disciples, Kumayl ibn Ziyad, outside Kufa to a graveyard, where he imparts the following private counsel in measured and deliberate tones:

O Kumayl ibn Ziyad, truly these hearts are vessels, and the best of them are those which hold the most. Retain from me that which I say to you. People are divided into three types: a lordly knower, one who seeks knowledge (⟪ar:متعلم⟫) for the sake of deliverance, and the common folk following just anyone, swaying with every current, not desiring to be illumined by the light of knowledge, nor seeking refuge from any strong support.

O Kumayl, knowledge is better than wealth, for knowledge guards you, while you must guard wealth; and wealth diminishes as it is spent, while knowledge increases as it is disbursed; and the results of wealth disappear with the disappearance of wealth. O Kumayl ibn Ziyad, the wise apprehension of knowledge is a religion by which Allah is worshipped. Through it, man acquires obedience in his life and a good name after his death. And knowledge is a judge, while wealth is judged.[^cite-95]

O Kumayl ibn Ziyad, those who hoard wealth perish even while they live, but the knowers endure for as long as time subsists; their material forms are absent, but their spiritual images remain present in the hearts of others. Ah! what abundant knowledge is here—he gestures toward his breast—if only I could find one to bear it! Yes, I found one who was quick to understand, but he could not be trusted with it, exploiting the tools of religion for worldly gain, empowering himself with the graces of Allah against His slaves, and with His proofs against His friends. Then there was one obedient to the bearers of truth, yet lacking heartfelt insight; at the first appearance of obscurity, doubt was kindled in his heart. So alas, neither this one nor that! Nor one who is greedy for pleasure, submissive to passion, nor one obsessed with acquisition and accumulation—neither of them is a guardian of religion in any respect. They resemble nothing so much as grazing cattle! Thus does knowledge die with the death of its bearers.

But indeed! By Allah, the earth will never be empty of one (Imam) who establishes the proof of Allah whether overtly with publicity or fearfully in obscurity, lest Allah's proofs and elucidations come to naught. But such as these—how many are they and where? By Allah, they may be the smallest in number, but with Allah they are the greatest in rank. Through them Allah preserves His proofs and elucidations, so that they entrust them to their peers and sow them in the hearts of those resembling them.[^cite-96] Through them, knowledge penetrates the reality of insight. They rejoice in their intimacy with the spirit of certainty; they make easy what the extravagant find harsh; they befriend that by which the ignorant are estranged. With their bodies they keep company with the world, while their spirits are tied to the transcendent realm. They are the vicegerents of Allah on His earth, summoners to His religion. Ah! how I long to see them! Go now, Kumayl, as you will.

---

## The Four Sentences on the Lordly Knower

For our purposes here, it suffices to reflect upon the four sentences of this khutba (sermon), in which we are given the description of those who bear the title of "lordly knower" (⟪ar:فَعَالِمٌ رَبَّانِيٌّ⟫)—those who are fewest in number yet greatest in the sight of Allah, and through whom "His religion" is sustained. His religion, that is, the divine reality of religion, the pure essence of the faith, which is perfect and immutable, in contrast to "religion" in the ordinary sense, whose formal institutions are subject to human vicissitudes. It is by means of the knowledge of the true knowers that religion is true to itself, and it is only through such sages that religion truly "lives" in the world.

## Through Them, Knowledge Penetrates the Reality of Insight

As noted before, knowledge can remain merely abstract and far removed from the "reality of insight" (⟪ar:من فھم علم گوہر العلم⟫). Yet it contains a potential that calls out to be realized. It needs to be enlivened in order to "penetrate" into something deeper than the words and concepts articulating its own form—the immediately intelligible meaning on the surface. Degrees of knowledge are clearly implied here, as is the avoidance of all literalism and superficiality, the facile equation between factual information and actual knowledge, let alone wisdom.[^cite-97] The shift of consciousness required for the "penetration" of knowledge is indicated graphically by the Commander's gesture of pointing to his breast.

The word "penetrate" translates the Arabic *hajama*, whose primary meaning is "to assault," "to take by storm," "to charge." In this context, the word helps us perceive that there is in conceptual knowledge a latent force—one might almost say an "explosive" power—deriving from the reality of knowledge, its *haqiqa* (spiritual essence). It is this which calls out to be "made real," through *tahaqquq* (investigation), that is, realization in depth, not merely factual verification on the surface. This *haqiqa* can be indicated, but not captured, by the words and concepts which constitute its formal communicable aspect. By means of this inner force, which its outer conceptual form both hides and evokes, it is capable of effecting a shift of consciousness from one degree of knowledge to another.

An indirect, mental conception of reality thus paves the way for a "penetrating," existential perception of reality. The "reality of insight" opens up the intelligence to reality as such, whereas ordinary insight, like abstract knowledge, remains only at the superficial level of understanding. The same idea is expressed in a saying of the Commander relating to true understanding, which comes to plumb and thus truly "know" the content of knowledge, its deeper dimensions or "buried treasures": "He who understands, knows the depth of knowledge (⟪ar:من فھم علم گوہر العلم⟫)." The effort required to attain true knowledge, as opposed to the mere accumulation of facts or details, is likewise indicated in this saying: "Through learning and assimilating knowledge [such that it becomes deeply rooted in one's consciousness], knowledge is attained (⟪ar:بالتَّعَلُّم یَنَالَ العِلمْ⟫)."[^cite-98]

The urgency of the need to realize the immanent power within knowledge—to move from the abstract to the concrete, the potential to the actual, the static to the dynamic, the formal to the essential—is effectively evoked by the use of the word *hajama*, which is related to a battle. It alerts one to the dangers of taking the initial understanding of an idea for its full comprehension, and full intellectual comprehension for its dynamic interiorization in spirituality. This interiorization is a process of infinite unfolding within, not the simple registering of some fact from without. It thus requires the effective integration of knowledge within being; knowledge from without needs to be transformed into being from within, woven into the deepest texture of one's existence. Thus, knowledge demands not just the attention of the mind but the total orientation of the soul.

In this ethos, then, the effective integration of knowledge (*tawhīd*, the unification principle) within being is unattainable without the participation of the whole soul. This, in turn, requires the elimination of all vices that flow from egocentricity. Thus, the *ʿāqil* (the true intellectual) is described by the Commander as being constantly engaged in "struggle against his own soul," against the lower inclinations, the passionate whims, and distracting caprices of the ego. All of these vices, far from being confined to the plane of morality, encroach upon the domain of knowledge; this will be understood in the very measure that knowledge is grasped as a concrete vision of things, and not just a notional or conceptual understanding. The vices are then regarded as so many clouds obscuring the vision proper to the "reality of insight." According to the Quran, evil actions become "rust" that covers over the heart:

⟪quran 83:14⟫  
⟪ar:‏كَلَّا ۖ بَلْۜ رَانَ عَلَىٰ قُلُوبِهِم مَّا كَانُوا۟ يَكْسِبُونَ ‎⟫[^cite-99]

The *ʿāqil* (intellectual), then, is engaged in constant inner struggle against the tendencies which not only sully the soul but also blind the heart. As a virtuous person, the *ʿāqil* opposes vice on the moral plane because it is contrary to virtue. But vice is also opposed for another reason, one situated on the higher level of spiritual reality: it contradicts the nature of the Real and obscures the reality of insight, blinding the "eye" of the heart which alone can "see" Allah.

It is not a question here of denying the validity of the moral perspective; rather, the stress is on the deeper motivation propelling the *ʿāqil* towards virtue. This motivation specifically transcends the moral plane while comprising it; morality emerges here as the fruit or the effect, and spirituality as the seed or the cause. By drawing attention to the intellectual aspect of the *ʿāqil*'s motivation for being rid of vices, the Commander brings out more clearly the way in which immorality is rooted in ignorance. The uprooting of this ignorance, for the true intellectual, is thus tantamount to dissolving the very substance of the vices. This uprooting requires great effort, a relentless battle within, which is indeed the most formidable of all battles—*al-jihād al-akbar* (the greatest struggle), as it was defined by the Prophet.

The Commander emphasizes the importance of this inner struggle in numerous sayings:

> Struggling against the soul through knowledge—such is the mark of the intellect.  
> The strongest people are those who are strongest against their own souls.  
> Truly, one who fights his own soul in obedience to Allah and does not sin against Him has the rank of the righteous martyr in Allah's eyes.  
> The ultimate battle is that of a man against his own soul.  
> He who knows his soul fights it.  
> No jihad is more excellent than the jihad of the soul.

In another saying, the Commander clarifies what the Prophet meant by defining the greatest jihad as that of the soul (*al-nafs*). It is not so much a question of fighting against the soul, but the battle **for** the soul itself. This emerges from the metaphor given by the Imam to define the struggle: *al-ʿaql* (the intellect) is the leader of the forces of *al-Rahmān* (the Compassionate); *al-hawā* (whim, caprice, desire) commands the forces of *al-shayṭān* (the devil). The soul (*al-nafs*) vacillates between them, susceptible to the attraction of both (⟪ar:مُتَجَاذِبَہ بینھما⟫) and enters into "the domain" of whichever of the two will triumph.[^cite-100]

These sayings underline the fact that, for the true intellectual, the soul in its entirety has to be "conquered" and enlisted on the side of the intellect. It is not a question of destroying the lower soul but of ridding it of the destructive influence of its caprice, its egoistic whims, subjectivism and individualism—all of these notions being connoted by the word *hawā*. The Prophet, we are told in the Quran, speaks not from *hawā*; rather:

⟪quran 53:3⟫  
⟪ar:‏وَمَا يَنطِقُ عَنِ ٱلْهَوَىٰٓ ‎⟫  
⟪quran 53:4⟫  
⟪ar:‏إِنْ هُوَ إِلَّا وَحْىٌۭ يُوحَىٰ ‎⟫[^cite-101]

The divine objectivity of revelation is here contrasted with the human subjectivity of passional whim. In the context of the holy war within, an analogous polarity is brought into focus by the Commander's sayings. The objectivity of the intellect must prevail against the subjectivity of individual caprice and egotistic desire. It is the intellect that brings to the forefront the divine power of *al-Rahmān* and which combats the forces of *al-shayṭān*, represented by *al-hawā*.

The word *hawā* is further used in the Quran in the sense of the desires stemming from the ego, by means of which the ego becomes a kind of false god:

⟪quran 25:43⟫  
⟪ar:‏أَرَءَيْتَ مَنِ ٱتَّخَذَ إِلَهَهُۥ هَوَىٰهُ أَفَأَنتَ تَكُونُ عَلَيْهِ وَكِيلًا ‎⟫[^cite-102]

This is the subtle or hidden idolatry (⟪ar:الشرک الخفی⟫) that the Prophet referred to as being more hidden than a black ant crawling on a black stone in a moonless night. To eliminate this idolatry of the ego, some force higher than that of the ego has to be engaged, for it is impossible for the ego to be controlled or dominated by something pertaining to the ego itself; and this higher force is the divine Reality, access to which is made possible through the power of the intellect and the graces of faith. This complementarity between the effort to overcome the egoism of *hawā* and the influx of divine grace is well expressed by the following verse of the Quran:

⟪quran 79:40⟫  
⟪ar:‏وَأَمَّا مَنْ خَافَ مَقَامَ رَبِّهِۦ وَنَهَى ٱلنَّفْسَ عَنِ ٱلْهَوَىٰ ‎⟫  
⟪quran 79:41⟫  
⟪ar:‏فَإِنَّ ٱلْجَنَّةَ هِىَ ٱلْمَأْوَىٰ ‎⟫[^cite-103]

The soul's fundamental energy is not to be destroyed but converted and redirected away from the transient objects of individualistic desire, away from the impulses of *al-shayṭān*, towards the one, true object that is expressed by *al-Rahmān*. This "conquest" of the soul involves much more than attaining to some mental conception. If one is to be rendered capable of perceiving that truth in all its dimensions and ramifications on the different planes of being, then it is not only the mind, the will and the character, but the fundamental disposition of the soul that needs to be integrated into the truth perceived by the intellect.

## The Spiritual Foundation: *Tawhīd* and Integration

It is important to stress that, for the Commander, the need to win the soul over in its totality follows naturally from his understanding of *tawhīd* (the principle of divine unity), the most fundamental principle of Islam. It is important not to lose sight of the fact that *tawhīd* is both a noun and a verb. It is often simply translated as "oneness" or "unity," but it literally means "affirming one" (the affirmation of and belief in the oneness of Allah, which is the outward, theological meaning) or "realizing the one" (the realization that there is **but** one reality, which is the inner, spiritual meaning). Thus, in the Commander's perspective, the absolutely Real is perceived not only as being one but also as making one, or integrating! The Real is not only unique; it is also unifying. *Tawhīd*, then, comes to mean not merely affirming the oneness of Allah but also "being" one in order to reflect perfectly the One; to know Allah is to be "integrated." One observes here a link with the etymology of the word *ʿaqal*, whose primary meaning is "to bind or restrain." Yet here one sees that the intellect is that which "binds together" the disparate elements of the soul into an organic unity. This principle is expressed in the following series of the Commander's sayings, each of which merits deep reflection:

> He who knows Allah integrates himself.  
> He who knows his soul disengages himself.  
> He who knows people isolates himself.  
> He who knows the world withholds himself.

Elsewhere this total adherence to the Real is referred to by the Commander as "*sincerity*," *ṣidq* or *ikhlāṣ*—terms which denote far more than simply truthfulness. The root of the word *ikhlas* is revealing: *khalusa* means to be utterly pure, true to one's essence, unadulterated by any extraneous substance. This aspect of the meaning is well expressed in the following saying of the Commander:

> Make purely for Allah your action and your knowledge, your love and your hatred, your taking and your leaving, your speech and your silence.

To be totally sincere, then, means to make oneself "purely" for Allah, to have no "stain" of impurity—here understood as what is other than Allah—present in one's devotion, action, intention and even sentiment. One must love and hate only in function of one's relationship with Allah. Of all the tendencies which insinuate themselves within one's motivations and intentions, the most pernicious is egotism. *Ikhlāṣ*, "pure sincerity," involves the active effort to BE, THINK and ACT purely for Allah, that is, with selflessness! Hence the stress placed by the Commander on the holy war within the soul as the "ultimate battle." One who succeeds in this battle attains the rank of the "righteous martyr." The victor here is the martyr because he has sacrificed his lower life for the sake of Allah, with whom is the real life, that which is witnessed (*shahida*) by the intellect, the only element within human consciousness capable of objectively looking upon the soul as "the other."

To repeat: "Struggling against the soul through knowledge—such is the mark of the intellect." The intellectual in this battle also bears "witness" (*al-shahīd*, meaning both martyr and witness). He bears witness to Allah through the intellect's sacrifice of that which opposes the truth of Allah, and the key opponent in question is his own egotism, his own "false life." To have perfect *ikhlāṣ*, then, is to be rid of all hypocritical self-preoccupation and to install in its place a sincere theocentricity, a mode of being "centered" upon Allah, not oneself. Thus, *ikhlāṣ* is to be for Allah or to belong to Allah, in the sense indicated by the Quranic verse:

⟪quran 6:162⟫  
⟪ar:‏قُلْ إِنَّ صَلَاتِى وَنُسُكِى وَمَحْيَاىَ وَمَمَاتِى لِلَّهِ رَبِّ ٱلْعَلَمِينَ ‎⟫[^cite-104]

The fact that worship is the first thing mentioned in this verse is of particular significance for anyone wishing to appreciate the nature of the Commander's spiritual *mizāj* (temperament, disposition). What must be stressed in the presentation of this ethos is the indispensable role performed by worship and devotion. One of the key distinguishing features of the Commander's spiritual ethos, indeed, is the stress placed by him on prayer, taking this term in its widest sense, to include all those means—from formal worship and supplication to meditation and reflection, remembrance and contemplation—by which the individual is oriented and attuned to the Real, *al-Haqq*.

Worship is, therefore, an integral aspect of *ikhlāṣ*, in the very measure that worship relates to and sustains a fundamental dimension of the human soul. That within the soul which yearns to worship and is satisfied only by the sustenance given through worship must be given its due. In a spiritual context, if worship is marginalized, one's sincerity is compromised, for one is not being sincere to Allah, not being "just" to Allah—not rendering Allah His due. And the spiritual logic of the Commander dictates that if one is not just with Allah, one cannot be just to His creatures. He says: "Whoever establishes well-being between himself and Allah, Allah establishes well-being between him and mankind."

One might also quote here the verse of the Quran:

⟪quran 15:99⟫  
⟪ar:‏وَٱعْبُدْ رَبَّكَ حَتَّىٰ يَأْتِيَكَ ٱلْيَقِينُ ‎⟫[^cite-105]

The quest for knowledge, then, demands total dedication if it is to yield up its inner riches, and this dedication includes that transformation of consciousness shaped by total concentration on the divine Reality—prayer in one of its deepest modes. In this form of prayer, the intensity of "dedication" is such that there is no room in one's consciousness for anything other than the object to which one is dedicated—the *dhikr* (remembrance) (⟪ar:ذکر⟫) of Allah strictly implying the forgetting of everything else, including, pre-eminently, oneself.[^cite-106] Concentration upon the divine Reality as the supreme object of knowledge, rendered present through the Name or Names of Allah, is thus a mode of prayer. And for it to be effective, it requires both presence of mind and a serious engagement with the will. It is far from sufficient to pray absent-mindedly in a mechanical fashion. In this connection the Commander says: "He who prays without exertion is like one who shoots arrows without a bow." Likewise, there is certainly nothing passive or easy about the process of acquiring knowledge: "Knowledge calls out for action; if it is answered, it is of avail; otherwise it departs."

The same idea is expressed by the Commander more forcefully in this saying: "When you hear knowledge being imparted, cleave to it; do not mix it up with jest, lest hearts spew it forth." Knowledge that is not taken seriously will be of no avail. It remains "undigested" and refuses to be assimilated by the "heart," the faculty that has access to the "reality of insight" and which can thus "see" Allah. In fact, it is not the individual who "assimilates" knowledge to himself, but knowledge—or rather, **the reality of being expressed by true knowledge**—that assimilates the individual: "Acquire knowledge, and true life will acquire you." The relationship between life and consciousness, so central to the intrinsic function of the spirit, is again being stressed in this maxim. At first, knowledge is sought by the individual and consciousness is cultivated; then, life "seeks" the individual as his being makes itself "real" or realizes itself more fully within the individual. In other words, the individual first makes an active effort to "integrate" knowledge within his being; but at a certain point, this activity is transformed into passive receptivity—being, "true life," integrates the individual within itself. To return to the Commander's guidance to Kumayl: "Through them knowledge penetrates the reality of insight." It is not the knowers who penetrate; it is knowledge that penetrates into the reality of insight, doing so through them, and this in the very measure of their effacement within knowledge, an effacement which is the shadow cast by their effective integration within the life that "acquires" them.

## They Rejoice in Their Intimacy with the Spirit of Certainty

The next sentence in the discourse to Kumayl is of particular importance in helping us to appreciate the sheer happiness that flows from the "life of the spirit." In the Arabic expression "⟪ar:وبشِّرُ روح الیقین⟫," the primary meaning of the word *bashara* means to have intimate experience of a thing, to come into direct physical contact with it. This form of the verb also relates to joy. Hence, "rejoice in intimate contact" better conveys something of the meaning of this single word. To touch the "spirit of certainty" is to come into contact, not with some cold logical fact, the conclusion of a purely mental process. Rather, this contact with the spirit of certainty means engaging deeply with the object of certainty, with that of which one is certain—ultimately, this being nothing less than the divine itself.[^cite-107] Since this reality is infinitely more beatific than any conceivable paradisal bliss—according to the Quran, the divine *riḍwān* (beatitude, divine contentment) (⟪ar:رِضوَان⟫) is "greater" than Paradise:

⟪quran 9:72⟫  
⟪ar:‏وَعَدَ ٱللَّهُ ٱلْمُؤْمِنِينَ وَٱلْمُؤْمِنَتِ جَنَّتٍۢ تَجْرِى مِن تَحْتِهَا ٱلْأَنْهَرُ خَلِدِينَ فِيهَا وَمَسَكِنَ طَيِّبَةًۭ فِى جَنَّتِ عَدْنٍۢ ۚ وَرِضْوَنٌۭ مِّنَ ٱللَّهِ أَكْبَرُ ۚ ذَلِكَ هُوَ ٱلْفَوْزُ ٱلْعَظِيمُ ‎⟫[^cite-108]

—it cannot but bestow joy upon the soul which effectively opens itself up to that reality. In the words of the Commander: "Learning and reflecting upon knowledge is the delight of the knowers." This is what one might call an "epistemology of joy," for "the most joyous of mankind is the intellectual (*al-ʿāqil*)." This joyous epistemology—which flies in the face of modern scientific conceptions of knowledge as dry, cold and abstract—should be considered carefully, as it reveals a crucial dimension of the spiritual knowledge proper to the Imam's perspective, and indeed, to all spiritual traditions worthy of the name. Coming into contact with truths should impart happiness, and this in the measure that the truths in question are fundamental to one's being or to Being as such.[^cite-109]

The two modes of being are ultimately identical, given the uniqueness, or the "one-and-onliness," of Being. That which has no second, no other, cannot enter the category of number; being is indivisible. Hence, the being of the individual cannot be different ultimately from Being as such, however great be the existential disparity—or rather, incommensurability—between the individual and Allah. True knowledge is knowledge of Being; thus it is perforce a kind of self-discovery, even if knowledge of the Absolute is impossible without absolute "self"-abandonment. The highest discovery calls for the deepest effacement.

However, in the initial stages of this process, true knowledge, acquired by the intellectual, is a kind of self-discovery that imparts the happiness of a homecoming, a return from the exile of ignorance. This follows because "the most intellectual of people" is "the one who is closest to Allah" and "the one who is most alive." To be close to Allah is to be fully alive, and this true life gives profound joy, the sheer joy of living. This is a joy proper to existence as such and is independent of particular experiences that may or may not be pleasurable.

Here again we see the crucial relationship between life and consciousness: the fruits, on one hand, of the "inspiration of the spirit of Allah," and on the other, between this life of spiritual consciousness and joy. Those who are true to the spirit of Allah within them truly possess joy. It is also by reason of the intimate and unshakeable certainty of the divine goodness and compassion that the true *faqīh*—not so much "the jurist" as "one who truly understands"—is defined by the Commander as "he who does not make people despair of the mercy of Allah, and does not make them lose hope in the gracious spirit of Allah." Such is the sage's state of immersion in the joyous certainty of Allah's mercy that there is no place for any despair. It is a sign of disbelief to despair of this mercy:

⟪quran 15:56⟫  
⟪ar:‏قَالَ وَمَن يَقْنَطُ مِن رَّحْمَةِ رَبِّهِۦٓ إِلَّا ٱلضَّآلُّونَ ‎⟫[^cite-110]

⟪quran 6:54⟫  
⟪ar:‏وَإِذَا جَآءَكَ ٱلَّذِينَ يُؤْمِنُونَ بِـَٔايَتِنَا فَقُلْ سَلَمٌ عَلَيْكُمْ ۖ كَتَبَ رَبُّكُمْ عَلَىٰ نَفْسِهِ ٱلرَّحْمَةَ ۖ أَنَّهُۥ مَنْ عَمِلَ مِنكُمْ سُوٓءًۢا بِجَهَلَةٍۢ ثُمَّ تَابَ مِنۢ بَعْدِهِۦ وَأَصْلَحَ فَأَنَّهُۥ غَفُورٌۭ رَّحِيمٌۭ ‎⟫[^cite-111]

"My mercy encompasses all things" and "Your Lord has prescribed for Himself mercy" are brought to life fully in the souls that have profound and not merely notional knowledge of the beatific nature of the divine Reality; they are the ones who truly "understand." They are the true *fuqahā* (the wise ones who comprehend the deeper dimensions of faith).

## Beauty, Excellence, and the Heart's Radiance

This feeling of joy through intimate knowledge of the mercy of Allah is inseparable from beauty. And the perception of this beauty is another natural association of the intellect in the Commander's perspective. The appreciation of beauty is not merely a question of subjective aesthetics; it is an objective aspect of the function of the intellect. Beauty also takes one to the heart of ethics. The Arabic *iḥsān* is often simply translated as "virtue" or "excellence." But one of its key literal meanings is "making beautiful"; *husn* means "beauty." Virtue, *iḥsān*, can thus be understood as that which "makes the soul beautiful." In this light, the relationship between the intellect, ethics and aesthetics is more clearly discerned: "The excellence of the intellect," says the Commander, "is in the beauty of things outward and inward (⟪ar:جمال الظواہر والبواطن⟫)."[^cite-112] The outward beauty of forms and actions must be complemented by inward beauty, that is, beauty of soul, or virtue in the most integral and penetrating sense of the word. All of this follows naturally from two sayings attributed to the Prophet: "Truly Allah is beautiful and He loves beauty" and "Assume the character traits of Allah."

Because Allah "loves beauty," this cultivation of "beautiful traits" within oneself makes one lovable to Allah. This process of making oneself lovable to Allah is inseparable from the emulation of the virtues brought to perfection in the soul of the Prophet:

⟪quran 3:31⟫  
⟪ar:‏قُلْ إِن كُنتُمْ تُحِبُّونَ ٱللَّهَ فَٱتَّبِعُونِى يُحْبِبْكُمُ ٱللَّهُ وَيَغْفِرْ لَكُمْ ذُنُوبَكُمْ ۗ وَٱللَّهُ غَفُورٌۭ رَّحِيمٌۭ ‎⟫[^cite-113]

This attraction of Allah's love through making oneself beautiful in the fullest sense is alluded to in another saying of the Commander: "The foundation of the intellect is the assimilation of love" (⟪ar:أَوَّلُ العَقل التَّوَدُّدْ⟫). Rather than simply referring to love as such (⟪ar:وُد/مَوَدَّۃ⟫), the Commander draws attention to the need to make oneself lovable to Allah by using the form *tawaddud*. This alludes to the process of integrating or realizing the principle of love within oneself, as well as expressing that love felt inside, outwardly. Love is thus appreciated both as outward radiance and inner attraction.[^cite-114]

To "follow" the Prophet and thus make oneself receptive to the divine influx of love means, among other things, cultivating the "prophetic" virtues. These virtues are prophetic not in the sense that they belong exclusively to the Prophet, but because he is deemed to have realized virtue in all its plenitude, serving as an example for his followers. It is thus not surprising to find that it is not only beauty and love that articulate the receptivity of the intellect to the divine Reality, but also such qualities as kindness and contentment, courtesy, generosity and modesty, wise forbearance. None of these moral qualities are absent from the soul of the true intellectual. There can be no wisdom or intellect in the fullest sense if the soul lacks these fundamental qualities. It is thanks to the presence of these virtues that the intellect is able to develop those dimensions of depth and breadth without which it remains confined to the flat, horizontal plane of its own operations. Without virtue it remains confined to the activity of reason alone, which until touched by faith and deepened by grace, remains unaware of what lies beneath the surface of its own field of activity. It remains oblivious of the "hidden treasures" of the intellect. It remains blind to the need to "integrate" the whole of the soul within the mould of that "integral," or "unifying" virtue which bestows integrity upon the intelligence. Such integration, or "making one," is absolutely fundamental to *tawhīd*.[^cite-115]

So *tawhīd* can now be understood as a dynamic process of **spiritual unification** that is demanded by the idea of the oneness of Allah. It is clearly necessary that truth must inspire the soul. But it is equally necessary that, conversely, virtue "aerate" the intelligence, provide it with the "leaven" (*khamīr*) which allows it to expand to its full dimensions.

## Vice as Spiritual Blindness

Sinful, immoral qualities are grasped in this perspective not only as vices but also as so many blind spots or intellectual dysfunctions. The heart is "diseased" by vice, its "eye" blinded by them and thus unable to "see" the Real. As is said in the Quran:

⟪quran 2:10⟫  
⟪ar:‏فِى قُلُوبِهِم مَّرَضٌۭ فَزَادَهُمُ ٱللَّهُ مَرَضًۭا ۖ وَلَهُمْ عَذَابٌ أَلِيمٌۢ بِمَا كَانُوا۟ يَكْذِبُونَ ‎⟫[^cite-116]

⟪quran 22:46⟫  
⟪ar:‏أَفَلَمْ يَسِيرُوا۟ فِى ٱلْأَرْضِ فَتَكُونَ لَهُمْ قُلُوبٌۭ يَعْقِلُونَ بِهَآ أَوْ آذَانٌۭ يَسْمَعُونَ بِهَا ۖ فَإِنَّهَا لَا تَعْمَى ٱلْأَبْصَرُ وَلَكِن تَعْمَى ٱلْقُلُوبُ ٱلَّتِى فِى ٱلصُّدُورِ ‎⟫[^cite-117]

As noted above, the vices are all comprised within egotism and this, in turn, is often described simply as "caprice" (⟪ar:ھوی⟫). In one saying, the Commander refers to this conquest over caprice as being tantamount to salvation: "He attains deliverance whose intellect dominates his caprice." Deliverance—*falāh*, which comprises the sense both of salvation and beatitude—is thus, to some degree, accessible here and now. It is not just a grace granted after one's demise.[^cite-118] This deliverance involves tasting the joy which emanates from certainty, from intimate contact with the spirit of this certainty.

As to the means of cultivating receptivity to this spirit of certainty, what is required, over and above obedience to the rules and regulations of the revealed Law, is that combination of purity of intention and sincerity of devotion which is expressed by the word *ikhlāṣ*. Without this sincerity or purity, complying with those formal rules is nothing but hypocrisy (*nifāq*) and pretension (*riyāʾ*), which, in turn, is defined by the Commander as the gravest of all sins—*shirk* (polytheism), the attribution of "partners" to Allah: "Know that the slightest pretension (*riyāʾ*) is polytheism." This sentence can be taken as a comment upon one of the most important condemnations of hypocrisy found in the Quran, in the verses that refer to those who deny the essence of the faith even while outwardly conforming to it:

⟪quran 107:1⟫  
⟪ar:‏أَرَءَيْتَ ٱلَّذِى يُكَذِّبُ بِٱلدِّينِ ‎⟫  
⟪quran 107:2⟫  
⟪ar:‏فَذَلِكَ ٱلَّذِى يَدُعُّ ٱلْيَتِيمَ ‎⟫  
⟪quran 107:3⟫  
⟪ar:‏وَلَا يَحُضُّ عَلَىٰ طَعَامِ ٱلْمِسْكِينِ ‎⟫  
⟪quran 107:4⟫  
⟪ar:‏فَوَيْلٌۭ لِّلْمُصَلِّينَ ‎⟫  
⟪quran 107:5⟫  
⟪ar:‏ٱلَّذِينَ هُمْ عَن صَلَاتِهِمْ سَاهُونَ ‎⟫  
⟪quran 107:6⟫  
⟪ar:‏ٱلَّذِينَ هُمْ يُرَآءُونَ ‎⟫  
⟪quran 107:7⟫  
⟪ar:‏وَيَمْنَعُونَ ٱلْمَاعُونَ ‎⟫[^cite-119]

This short sura makes it clear that to be "religious" in one's formal acts of devotion has no value whatsoever unless these acts be integrated into a life of virtue and unless one is performing one's devotion with the right intention, that is, with *ikhlāṣ*. It also helps us to see the reason for not referring to the Commander's ethos as simply "religious" but as "spiritual," taking the ideals of religion to their most profound application. Without this dimension of depth, religion is meaningless.[^cite-120]

"There is no religion for one who has no intellect," says the Commander. Likewise, "Action without knowledge is error." The intention motivating the action is what counts. For "action—all action—is dust, except what is purified [accomplished sincerely] within it." This detachment from action as such and the orientation instead towards an inner state of being, one from which good actions cannot but emerge, clearly reflects what is found in the Quran:

⟪quran 22:37⟫  
⟪ar:‏لَن يَنَالَ ٱللَّهَ لُحُومُهَا وَلَا دِمَآؤُهَا وَلَكِن يَنَالُهُ ٱلتَّقْوَىٰ مِنكُمْ ۚ كَذَلِكَ سَخَّرَهَا لَكُمْ لِتُكَبِّرُوا۟ ٱللَّهَ عَلَىٰ مَا هَدَىٰكُمْ ۗ وَبَشِّرِ ٱلْمُحْسِنِينَ ‎⟫[^cite-121]

## The Intellect and Spiritual Certainty

"There is no religion for one who has no intellect"—this principle was exemplified in striking manner by the *Khārijites*, the group that rebelled against the Commander. Among this group were numerous "readers" (*qurrāʾ*) of the Quran, people who possessed considerable religious prestige on account of their knowledge of the Quran and their intense worship. Their piety, however, was more apparent than real; it was, in fact, a "zeal of bitterness," an imitation of true piety. When his supporters made mention of the long night vigils performed by members of this group, the Commander gave this sharp reply: "Sleeping with certainty is better than praying with doubt." Certainty, as we have seen, imparts peace and joy. Thus sleep, for one who is immersed in what the Commander calls "the spirit of certainty," is a bodily repose which further deepens the happiness that flows from a good conscience and spiritual certitude. This spiritual certitude is understood as a kind of permanent "wakefulness" in the sense of being awake to the higher realities. Thus, bodily sleep does not contradict spiritual wakefulness. As the hadith of the Prophet states: "My eyes sleep, but my heart sleeps not."[^cite-122]

In relation to formal worship, the true intellectual is one who is obedient to the divine injunction to pray. But rather than praying in mechanical fashion and believing that the action alone suffices, the *ʿāqil* knows that such obedience is a necessary, but not sufficient, condition for achieving receptivity to grace. There must be consciousness of what one is doing and why one is doing it. And it is this awareness, together with virtue, or "inward beauty," that attracts the divine acceptance. "The most worthy knowledge for you," says the Commander, "is that by virtue of whose presence, action is accepted by Allah."

## They Make Easy What the Extravagant Find Harsh

This passage now takes us, among other things, to the impact made on the soul of the true sages by recollection of the inevitability of death in this world and the consequent necessity of renunciation within it. In the first place, that which is found to be "harsh" by those who are extravagant—the avid, worldly types—is outward adversity, difficulty, deprivation. These are all circumstances which, in the Quran, are viewed as the trials which inevitably accompany life in this world:

⟪quran 2:155⟫  
⟪ar:‏وَلَنَبْلُوَنَّكُم بِشَىْءٍۢ مِّنَ ٱلْخَوْفِ وَٱلْجُوعِ وَنَقْصٍۢ مِّنَ ٱلْأَمْوَلِ وَٱلْأَنفُسِ وَٱلثَّمَرَتِ ۗ وَبَشِّرِ ٱلصَّبِرِينَ ‎⟫  
⟪quran 2:156⟫  
⟪ar:‏ٱلَّذِينَ إِذَآ أَصَبَتْهُم مُّصِيبَةٌۭ قَالُوٓا۟ إِنَّا لِلَّهِ وَإِنَّآ إِلَيْهِ رَجِعُونَ ‎⟫  
⟪quran 2:157⟫  
⟪ar:‏أُو۟لَٓئِكَ عَلَيْهِمْ صَلَوَتٌۭ مِّن رَّبِّهِمْ وَرَحْمَةٌۭ ۖ وَأُو۟لَٓئِكَ هُمُ ٱلْمُهْتَدُونَ ‎⟫[^cite-123]

The wise, the rightly-guided (*al-muhtadūn*), perceive the "blessings" that are showered down upon those who are patient in the face of afflictions. This patience is a result of the completeness of their knowledge of the truth.

⟪quran 103:1⟫  
⟪ar:‏وَٱلْعَصْرِ ‎⟫  
⟪quran 103:2⟫  
⟪ar:‏إِنَّ ٱلْإِنسَنَ لَفِى خُسْرٍ ‎⟫  
⟪quran 103:3⟫  
⟪ar:‏إِلَّا ٱلَّذِينَ آمَنُوا۟ وَعَمِلُوا۟ ٱلصَّلِحَتِ وَتَوَاصَوْا۟ بِٱلْحَقِّ وَتَوَاصَوْا۟ بِٱلصَّبْرِ ‎⟫[^cite-124]

These are the believers who remain steadfast in the face of trials, being firmly rooted in their awareness of their origin and end, and the fact that "unto Him we are ever-returning." For this reason, they can "make easy" the trials and difficulties of life, whereas the "extravagant" experience them solely in their aspect of harshness and bitterness. The "ease" that permanently exists in all difficulty is hidden from the ignorant but discerned by the wise:

⟪quran 94:5⟫  
⟪ar:‏فَإِنَّ مَعَ ٱلْعُسْرِ يُسْرًا ‎⟫  
⟪quran 94:6⟫  
⟪ar:‏إِنَّ مَعَ ٱلْعُسْرِ يُسْرًۭا ‎⟫[^cite-125]

The wise go even further than just anticipating "ease" as the inevitable fruit of hardship. They taste this fruit already as the seed hidden within hardship itself, for they actually "befriend that by which the ignorant are estranged."

We can understand one level of meaning here with the help of this saying of the Commander: "Death is more intimate to me than the breast of the mother is to the suckling baby." For in the verse cited above, the mention of trials and difficulties is preceded by a reminder that those who are slain "in the path of Allah" are not really dead; "No! they are alive but you do not perceive." We have here not simply an affirmation of the life hereafter, but also an allusion to the esoteric meaning of being "slain" for Allah, *al-fanāʾ fī-Allāh* (extinction in Allah) (⟪ar:الفنا فی اللہ⟫), or annihilation of the self in the divine.[^cite-126] This intimacy with death, mentioned by the Commander, can be seen to relate to this "death" while still alive, which he also alludes to in another saying: "I am the death of the dead." It is because life in the world itself is a kind of death—a limitation. This life is privation, in contrast to the infinite abundance of the divine realms. Therefore, death in this world is tantamount to true life. As the Quran says:

⟪quran 29:64⟫  
⟪ar:‏وَمَا هَذِهِ ٱلْحَيَوٰةُ ٱلدُّنْيَآ إِلَّا لَهْوٌۭ وَلَعِبٌۭ ۚ وَإِنَّ ٱلدَّارَ ٱلْآخِرَةَ لَهِىَ ٱلْحَيَوَانُ ۚ لَوْ كَانُوا۟ يَعْلَمُونَ ‎⟫[^cite-127]

However, it is important to stress that it is not the physical world as such that is to be negated, rather it is worldliness. It is the egotistic attachment to this world that must be overcome. Here we need to dwell a little on one of the key principles of the Commander's perspective, which is stressed repeatedly throughout the Nahj al-Balāgha (Path of Eloquence). This is the principle of *zuhd* (asceticism, detachment), which cannot be translated by any single word in English. It comprises several meanings: asceticism, austerity, abstention, detachment, doing without, making little of. The following passage from the Nahj al-Balāgha expresses well the attitude of the *zāhid* (the one who practices detachment) toward this world:

> The world is an abode for which annihilation is ordained, and for its people departure from it is decreed. It is sweet and green, and it hastens to one who seeks it. It confounds the heart that gazes upon it. So set out from it with the best of the provision available to you, and ask not from it above what suffices and seek not from it more than what can be attained.

What is this "best of provision" (*zād*)? In many places in the Nahj, it is described as *taqwā* (piety, God-consciousness), another word which is difficult to translate. The meaning of *taqwā* is: Piety, Fear of Allah, Awe, and the consciousness of Allah. "I strongly encourage you, O slaves of Allah, to *taqwā*, which is the true provision," says the Commander. This provision for the next world is only attained in the measure of one's detachment from the perishable aspects of the "life of this world." The Quran states:

⟪quran 17:18⟫  
⟪ar:‏مَّن كَانَ يُرِيدُ ٱلْعَاجِلَةَ عَجَّلْنَا لَهُۥ فِيهَا مَا نَشَآءُ لِمَن نُّرِيدُ ثُمَّ جَعَلْنَا لَهُۥ جَهَنَّمَ يَصْلَىٰهَا مَذْمُومًۭا مَّدْحُورًۭا ‎⟫[^cite-128]

The despicable aspect of this world is described in vivid terms by the Commander:

> By Allah, this world of yours is more contemptible in my eyes than a chewed-up bone of a pig in the hand of a leper.  
> The image of this world is that of a snake—soft to touch but filled with virulent poison.  
> This world is as worthless to me as that which drips from the nose of a goat.

It is the grandeur and beauty of Allah that render this world insignificant and distasteful. It is only in the light of the Absolute that the pleasures and attractions of the world appear fleeting and illusory: "The greatness of the Creator for you belittles creatures in your view." In similar vein, the Commander refers to a "brother in Allah": "What made him great in my eyes was the smallness of the world in his eyes."

This, according to the Quranic usage of *zuhd*, is the real meaning of detachment. In Surah Yusuf (Chapter of Joseph), the brothers of Yusuf "deemed him of little value" in selling him for a small price:

⟪quran 12:20⟫  
⟪ar:‏وَشَرَوْهُ بِثَمَنٍۭ بَخْسٍۢ دَرَهِمَ مَعْدُودَةٍۢ وَكَانُوا۟ فِيهِ مِنَ ٱلزَّهِدِينَ ‎⟫[^cite-129]

They were described as being, in relation to him, "*zāhidīn*" (those who regard as insignificant). The world, in the Commander's perspective, is likewise deemed of little value. Not that it is altogether valueless, but that, in relation to the Hereafter, in relation to eternity, in relation to the ultimate reality, it is rendered all but insignificant. All created things are reduced to infinitesimal, fleeting shadows, destined for annihilation:

⟪quran 28:88⟫  
⟪ar:‏وَلَا تَدْعُ مَعَ ٱللَّهِ إِلَهًا آخَرَ ۘ لَآ إِلَهَ إِلَّا هُوَ ۚ كُلُّ شَىْءٍ هَالِكٌ إِلَّا وَجْهَهُۥ ۚ لَهُ ٱلْحُكْمُ وَإِلَيْهِ تُرْجَعُونَ ‎⟫[^cite-130]

However, the world is not deprived of all value. All things proclaim the infinite creativity of the Creator:

⟪quran 17:44⟫  
⟪ar:‏تُسَبِّحُ لَهُ ٱلسَّمَوَتُ ٱلسَّبْعُ وَٱلْأَرْضُ وَمَن فِيهِنَّ ۚ وَإِن مِّن شَىْءٍ إِلَّا يُسَبِّحُ بِحَمْدِهِۦ وَلَكِن لَّا تَفْقَهُونَ تَسْبِيحَهُمْ ۗ إِنَّهُۥ كَانَ حَلِيمًا غَفُورًۭا ‎⟫[^cite-131]

To help us imaginatively comprehend the mystery of this universal praise, the Quran tells us:

⟪quran 24:41⟫  
⟪ar:‏أَلَمْ تَرَ أَنَّ ٱللَّهَ يُسَبِّحُ لَهُۥ مَن فِى ٱلسَّمَوَتِ وَٱلْأَرْضِ وَٱلطَّيْرُ صَٓفَّتٍۢ ۖ كُلٌّۭ قَدْ عَلِمَ صَلَاتَهُۥ وَتَسْبِيحَهُۥ ۗ وَٱللَّهُ عَلِيمٌۢ بِمَا يَفْعَلُونَ ‎⟫[^cite-132]

It is thus not at all contradictory for the Commander to both despise the world and yet express, in his description, the beauties of virgin nature. This includes that unsurpassable marvel, the peacock, described in a sermon which includes depictions of birds in general, mentioning also ants, serpents and elephants. It is noteworthy that the sermon concludes with an exciting description of Paradise, comparing the marvels of this world with the delights of the next. When compared to the latter, all terrestrial phenomena pale into insignificance. For once the "eye of the heart" is directed to Paradise, "your soul would become averse to the marvels of this world—its passions, its pleasures and its embellished scenery."

It might seem contradictory to bring together such apparently opposed qualities as joy in certainty, discussed above, and the "befriending" of hardship. But the latter is in fact the fruit of the former. All difficulties without exception are endured, thanks to the spiritual resources that are made available by faith, combined with intellectual assimilation—rather, in the measure that faith is intellectually assimilated and thus becomes *maʿrifa* (gnosis). This gnosis comprises a certainty which is so deep and so complete that "even if the veil be lifted" it cannot increase, according to the famous saying of the Commander: ⟪ar:لو کشف الغطاء ما ازدت یقینا⟫.[^cite-133] Even if all the veils [of this world] were lifted [from my eyes], it would not increase my certainty in the least bit.

It is possible to believe, at one level of the mind, that "there is no deity but Allah." The Commander, however, draws attention to what this apparently simple belief should imply when it is "sincere" or "pure." "I bear witness that there is no deity but Allah alone Who has no partner—a testimony whose sincerity has been tested and whose essence has been assimilated with conviction (⟪ar:مُعتَقَداً مُصَعصَھَا⟫)." The word *muṣāṣ* refers to the choicest and purest part of any substance, that which constitutes its essence. The root of the word *masaṣa* means "to suck"; the idea being that the liquid or juice of a substance or fruit contains its essence, that which is "sucked" out of it.[^cite-134] In the present context, the Commander is stressing the spiritual substance of his testimony, as opposed to the mere verbal affirmation or mental comprehension of the words. He affirms a heartfelt connection with the One for whom his testimony bears witness. The words defining the testimony echo its reality. The words are not to be confused with that reality itself.

Going back to the theme of "befriending that by which the ignorant are estranged," one might say that this implies not just the trials of this life, which are a source of strength for the wise and repulsion for the ignorant, but also the inescapability of death. For the wise, this encounter with Allah for a blissful eternity means a release. In other words, a mode of *tawhīd* whereby the soul enjoys ever greater degrees of integration within higher principles. For the ignorant, by contrast, death is perceived as a frightful disintegration. It means the end of their world, rather than the beginning of an endless life. This goes back to the earlier concept of death [while still going through] life, described as *fanāʾ* (extinction, effacement), the "perishing" of egoistic consciousness in the face of the Absolute. The ignorant shrink in horror at the prospect of the loss of what is falsely taken to be "their" life. They do not realize that this sacrifice of egoistic and limited self-consciousness is the very basis on which infinite consciousness is glimpsed or "tasted." One can only "taste" the ocean of the Real by being drowned in it. One could even say that the means to this ultimate attainment—the act of pure prayer, total concentration on the Real—is also "embraced" by the wise and feared by the ignorant. For this concentration strictly precludes all worldly or egotistic preoccupations. The very intensity of the concentration upon the Absolute burns away thoughts of the relative. For those who ignorantly identify with relativities, both inward and outward, this implies a kind of death. For the wise, by contrast, this concentration on the Absolute—the unsullied *dhikr* (remembrance) of Allah—is a joyous contemplation of reality before which the ego is displaced and all illusions are dispelled.

## With Their Bodies, They Keep Company with the World

This sentence can be more concretely understood if it is read in the light of another well-known expression of the Commander. He describes himself as belonging to a group whose degree of spirituality is such that "their hearts are in the Gardens [of Paradise] while their bodies are at work." The fact of being "tied" or "attached to that realm," consisting of the paradisal abodes right up to the divine Presence itself, implies then that they are already "within" that transcendent realm more than they are in this earthly one.[^cite-135]

This helps explain the saying quoted earlier: Allah keeps man perpetually in that which his soul desires. Those whose hearts or spirits are "in" the transcendent domain are those whose consciousness is substantially (and not merely conceptually) determined by the transcendent realities. Those whose consciousness is determined by worldly matters are "in" this world, in the sense of being enclosed by its formal limitations. Their orientation and imagination are trapped within a worldly framework. Their actions bear the mark of this "worldliness," whereas the actions of the wise are the fruits of their immersion in a higher reality. One might say that their outward actions in the world disguise their inward transcendence of all action. They ACT, but are not bound by the fruits of their action. As stated earlier, they are aware that "action—all action—is dust, except what is purified (accomplished sincerely) within."

## The Hereafter as Spiritual Reality

At this point it would be appropriate to dwell briefly on the role played by the notion of the Hereafter within the ethical framework of the Commander. As we saw earlier and will see in what follows, the Hereafter is repeatedly invoked or evoked in the discourses of the Commander. Is it a question of offering a heavenly carrot and threatening a hellish stick, in order simply to "enjoin the good and forbid the evil"? Even if we believe that the answer is affirmative on one level, there is a deeper side to this seemingly simplistic utilization of the ethical import of divine warnings and promises on human action.

The point of view adopted in regard to ethical orientation here is defined essentially by the spiritual reality not just of Allah but also of the Hereafter. Both objectively, in itself, and subjectively—that is, insofar as it is assimilated by the individual. It thus becomes a dynamic dimension—a process, orientation or reflux—within the very soul of the believer, rather than a mere belief or hope. Anticipation of the Hereafter is one thing, but its realization is completely another. And it is this realization that characterizes the soul of the "friend" of Allah (*walī Allāh*), the saint and the sage.

As noted earlier, the Commander says in a famous sentence which bears witness to his state of total immersion within the reality of which he is certain: "Were the veil [of this world] to be removed I would not increase in certitude (⟪ar:لو کش
